55sn (25:34) The style here is typical of Hebrew narrative; after the tension is resolved with the dialogue, the working out of it is recorded in a rapid sequence of verbs ("gave"; "ate"; "drank"; "got up"; "went out"). See also Gen. 3:1-7 for another example.
56sn (25:34) So Esau despised his birthright. This clause, which concludes the episode, is a summary statement which reveals the underlying significance of Esau's actions. "To despise" means to treat something as worthless or with contempt. Esau's willingness to sell his birthright was evidence that he considered it to be unimportant.
1tn (26:1) Heb "in addition to the first famine which was."
2sn (26:1) This account is parallel to two similar stories about Abraham (see Gen 12:10-20; 20:1-18). Many scholars do not believe there were three similar incidents, only one that got borrowed and duplicated. Many regard the account about Isaac as the original, which then was attached to the more important person, Abraham, with supernatural elements being added. For a critique of such an approach, see R. Alter, The Art of Biblical Narrative, 47-62. It is more likely that the story illustrates the proverb "like father, like son" (see T. W. Mann, The Book of the Torah, 53). In typical human fashion the son follows his father's example of lying to avoid problems. The appearance of similar events reported in a similar way underscores the fact that the blessing has now passed to Isaac, even if he fails as his father did.
3sn (26:2) Do not go down to Egypt. The words echo Gen 12:10, which reports that "Abram went down to Egypt," but state the opposite.
4tn (26:2) Heb "say to you."
5tn (26:3) The Hebrew verb rWG (GWr) means "to live temporarily without ownership of land." Abraham's family will not actually possess the land of Canaan until the Israelite conquest hundreds of years later.
6tn (26:3) After the imperative "stay" the two prefixed verb forms with prefixed conjunction here indicate consequence.
sn (26:3) I will be with you and I will bless you. The promise of divine presence is a promise to intervene to protect and to bless.
7tn (26:3) The Hebrew term ur~z\ (z\r^u) occurring here and in v. 18 may mean "seed" (for planting), "offspring" (occasionally of animals, but usually of people), or "descendants" depending on the context.
sn (26:3) To you and to your descendants. The Abrahamic blessing will pass to Isaac. Everything included in that blessing will now belong to the son, and in turn will be passed on to his sons. But there is a contingency involved: if they are to enjoy the full blessings, they will have to obey the word of the LORD. And so obedience is enjoined here, with the example of how well Abraham obeyed.
8tn (26:3) The Hiphil stem of the verb <Wq (qW<) here means "to fulfill; to bring to realization." For other examples of this use of this verb form, see Lev 26:9; Num 23:19; Deut 8:18; 9:5; 1 Sam 1:23; 1 Kgs 6:12; Jer 11:5.
9tn (26:3) Heb "the oath which I swore."
sn (26:3) The solemn promise I made. See Gen 15:18-20; 22:16-18.
10tn (26:4) Heb "your descendants."
11tn (26:4) Traditionally the verb is taken as passive ("will be blessed") here, as if Abraham's descendants were going to be a channel or source of blessing to the nations. But the Hitpael is better understood here as reflexive/reciprocal, "will bless [i.e., pronounce blessings on] themselves/one another" (see also Gen 22:18). Elsewhere the Hitpael of the verb "to bless" is used with a reflexive/reciprocal sense in Deut 29:18; Ps 72:17; Isa 65:16; Jer 4:2. Gen 12:2 predicts that Abram will be held up as a paradigm of divine blessing and that people will use his name in their blessing formulae. For examples of blessing formulae utilizing an individual as an example of blessing see Gen 48:20 and Ruth 4:11. Earlier formulations of this promise (see Gen 12:2; 18:18) use the Niphal stem. (See also Gen 28:14.)
12tn (26:5) The words "All this will come to pass" are not in the Hebrew text, but are supplied for stylistic reasons.
13tn (26:5) Heb "listened to my voice."
14sn (26:5) My charge, my commandments, my statutes, and my laws. The language of this verse is clearly interpretive, for Abraham did not have all these laws. The terms are legal designations for sections of the Mosaic law and presuppose the existence of the law. Some Rabbinic views actually conclude that Abraham had fulfilled the whole law before it was given (see the Mishnah, m. Qiddushin 4:14). Some scholars argue that this story could only have been written after the law was given (C. Westermann, Genesis, 2:424-25). But the simplest explanation is that the narrator (traditionally taken to be Moses the Lawgiver) elaborated on the simple report of Abraham's obedience by using terms with which the Israelites were familiar. In this way he depicts Abraham as the model of obedience to God's commands, whose example Israel should follow.
15sn (26:7) Rebekah, unlike Sarah, was not actually her husband's sister.
16tn (26:7) Heb "lest." The words "for he thought to himself" are supplied because the next clause is written with a first person pronoun, showing that Isaac was saying or thinking this.
17tn (26:7) Heb "kill me on account of."
18tn (26:8) Heb "and he"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
19tn (26:8) Heb "and it happened when the days were long to him there."
20tn (26:8) Heb "look, Isaac." By the use of the particle hN}h! (h!N@h, "look"), the narrator invites the audience to view the scene through Abimelech's eyes.
21tn (26:8) Or "fondling."
sn (26:8) The Hebrew word m=x^j@q (qj@x^m=), from the root x*j*q (qjx), "laugh," forms a soundplay with the name "Isaac" right before it. Here it depicts an action, probably caressing or fondling, that indicated immediately that Rebekah was Isaac's wife, not his sister. Isaac's deception made a mockery of God's covenantal promise. Ignoring God's promise to protect and bless him, Isaac lied to protect himself and acted in bad faith to the men of Gerar.
22tn (26:9) Heb "Surely, look!" See N. H. Snaith, "The meaning of Hebrew `ak," VT 14 (1964): 221-25.
23tn (26:9) Heb "Because I said, `Lest I die on account of her.'" Since the verb "said" probably means "said to myself" (i.e., "thought") here, the direct discourse in the Hebrew statement has been converted to indirect discourse in the translation. In addition the simple prepositional phrase "on account of her" has been clarified in the translation as "to get her" (cf. v. 7).
24tn (26:10) Heb "What is this you have done to us?" The Hebrew demonstrative pronoun "this" adds emphasis: "What in the world have you done to us?" (Williams, Hebrew Syntax, §118).
25tn (26:10) Heb "people."
26tn (26:10) The Hebrew verb means "to lie down." Here the expression "lie with" or "sleep with" is euphemistic for "have sexual relations with."
27tn (26:11) Heb "strikes." Here the verb has the nuance "to harm in any way." It would include assaulting the woman or killing the man.
28tn (26:11) The use of the infinitive absolute before the imperfect makes the construction emphatic.
29tn (26:12) Heb "a hundredfold."
30tn (26:12) This final clause explains why Isaac had such a bountiful harvest.
31tn (26:13) Heb "great." In this context the statement refers primarily to Isaac's material wealth, although reputation and influence are included.
32tn (26:13) Heb "and he went, going and becoming great." The construction stresses that his growth in possessions and power continued steadily.
33tn (26:14) Heb "and there was to him."
34tn (26:14) Heb "possessions of sheep."
35tn (26:14) Heb "possessions of cattle."
36tn (26:14) The Hebrew verb translated "became jealous" refers here to intense jealousy or envy that leads to hostile action (see v. 15).
37tn (26:15) Heb "and the Philistines stopped them up and filled them with dirt."
38tn (26:16) Heb "Go away from us."
39sn (26:16) You have become much more powerful. This explanation for the expulsion of Isaac from Philistine territory foreshadows the words used later by the Egyptians to justify their oppression of Israel (see Exod 1:9).
40tn (26:17) Heb "and he camped in the valley of Gerar and he lived there."
sn (26:17) This valley was actually a wadi (a dry river bed where the water would flow in the rainy season, but this would have been rare in the Negev). The water table under it would have been higher than in the desert because of water soaking in during the torrents, making it easier to find water when digging wells. However, this does not minimize the blessing of the LORD, for the men of the region knew this too, but did not have the same results.
41tn (26:18) Heb "he returned and dug," meaning "he dug again" or "he reopened."
42tn (26:18) Heb "that they dug." Since the subject is indefinite, the verb is translated as passive.
43tn (26:18) Heb "and the Philistines had stopped them up." This clause explains why Isaac had to reopen them.
44tn (26:18) Heb "and he"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
45tn (26:18) Heb "called names to them according to the names that his father called them."
46tn (26:19) Heb "living." This expression refers to a well supplied by subterranean streams (see Song 4:15).
47tn (26:20) The Hebrew verb translated "quarreled" describes a conflict that often has legal ramifications.
48tn (26:20) Heb "and he"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
49tn (26:20) Heb "and he called the name of the well."
50sn (26:20) The name Esek means "argument" in Hebrew. The following causal clause explains that Isaac gave the well this name as a reminder of the conflict its discovery had created. In the Hebrew text there is a wordplay, for the name is derived from the verb translated "argued."
51tn (26:20) The words "about it" are supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
52tn (26:21) Heb "and he called its name." The referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
53sn (26:21) The name Sitnah is derived from a Hebrew verbal root meaning "to oppose; to be an adversary." The name was a reminder that the digging of this well caused "opposition" from the Philistines.
54tn (26:22) Heb "and he"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
55tn (26:22) Heb "and he called its name."
56sn (26:22) The name Rehoboth is derived from a verbal root meaning "to make room." The name was a reminder that God had made room for them. The story shows Isaac's patience with the opposition; it also shows how God's blessing outdistanced the men of Gerar. They could not stop it or seize it any longer.
57tn (26:23) Heb "and he went up from there"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
58tn (26:25) Heb "called in the name of." The expression refers to worshiping the LORD through prayer and sacrifice (see Gen 4:26; 12:8; 13:4; 21:33). See G. Wenham, Genesis 1-15, 116.
59tn (26:25) Heb "and they dug there, the servants of Isaac, a well."
60tn (26:26) The disjunctive clause supplies pertinent supplemental information. The past perfect is used because the following narrative records the treaty at Beersheba. Prior to this we are told that Isaac settled in Beersheba; presumably this treaty would have allowed him to do that. However, it may be that he settled there and then made the treaty by which he renamed the place Beersheba. In this case one may translate "Now Abimelech came to him."
61tn (26:26) Heb "and."
62tn (26:26) Many modern translations render the Hebrew term u^r}m@ (m@r@u^) as "councilor" or "adviser," but the term may not designate an official position but simply a close personal friend.
63tn (26:27) The disjunctive clause is circumstantial, expressing the reason for his question.
64tn (26:28) The infinitive absolute before the verb emphasizes the clarity of their perception.
65tn (26:28) Heb "And we said, `Let there be.'" The direct discourse in the Hebrew text has been rendered as indirect discourse in the translation for stylistic reasons.
66tn (26:28) The pronoun "us" here is inclusive--it refers to the Philistine contingent on the one hand and Isaac on the other.
67tn (26:28) The pronoun "us" here is exclusive--it refers to just the Philistine contingent (the following "you" refers to Isaac).
68tn (26:28) The translation assumes that the cohortative expresses their request. Another option is to understand the cohortative as indicating resolve: "We want to make.'"
69tn (26:29) The oath formula is used: "if you do us harm" means "so that you will not do."
70tn (26:29) Heb "touched."
71tn (26:29) Heb "and just as we have done only good with you."
72tn (26:29) Heb "and we sent you away."
73tn (26:29) The Philistine leaders are making an observation, not pronouncing a blessing, so the translation reads "you are blessed" rather than "may you be blessed" (cf. NAB).
74tn (26:30) Heb "and he"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
75tn (26:30) Heb "and they ate and drank."
76tn (26:31) Heb "and they got up early and they swore an oath, a man to his brother."
77tn (26:31) Heb "and they went from him in peace."
78tn (26:32) Heb "and they said to him, `We have found water.'" The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
79sn (26:33) The name Shibah means (or at least sounds like) the word meaning "oath." The name was a reminder of the oath sworn by Isaac and the Philistines to solidify their treaty.
80sn (26:33) The name Beersheba (B=a@r v*b^u [ub^v* ra@B=]) means "well of an oath" or "well of seven." According to Gen 21:31 Abraham gave Beersheba its name when he made a treaty with the Philistines. Because of the parallels between this earlier story and the account in 26:26-33, some scholars see chaps. 21 and 26 as two versions (or doublets) of one original story. However, if one takes the text as it stands, it appears that Isaac made a later treaty agreement with the people of the land that was similar to his father's. Abraham dug a well at the site and named the place Beersheba; Isaac dug another well there and named the well Shibah. Later generations then associated the name Beersheba with Isaac, even though Abraham gave the place its name at an earlier time.
81tn (26:34) The sentence begins with the temporal indicator ("and it happened"), making this clause subordinate to the next.
82tn (26:34) Heb "the son of forty years."
83tn (26:34) Heb "took as a wife."
84tn (26:35) Heb "And they were [a source of ] bitterness in spirit to Isaac and to Rebekah."
1tn (27:1) The clause begins with the temporal indicator ("and it happened"), making it subordinate to the main clause that follows later in the sentence.
2tn (27:1) Heb "and his eyes were weak from seeing."
3tn (27:1) Heb "greater" (in terms of age).
4tn (27:1) Heb "he"; the referent (Esau) is specified in the translation for clarity.
5tn (27:2) Heb "he"; the referent (Isaac) is specified in the translation for clarity.
6tn (27:2) The particle hN}h! (h!N@h, "look") here introduces a logically foundational statement, upon which the coming instruction will be based.
7tn (27:2) Heb "I do not know the day of my death."
8tn (27:3) The Hebrew word is to be spelled either dy]x^ (x^y]d) following the marginal reading (Qere), or hd´yx@ (x@d*h), following the consonantal text (Kethib). Either way it is from the same root as the imperative hd´Wx (xWd*h, "hunt down").
9tn (27:4) Following the imperative, the cohortative (with the prefixed conjunction) indicates purpose or result.
10tn (27:4) Heb "so that my soul may bless you." The use of yv!p=n~ (n~pc!, "my soul") as the subject emphasizes that the blessing will be made with all Isaac's desire and vitality. The conjunction "so that" closely relates the meal to the blessing, suggesting that this will be a ritual meal in conjunction with the giving of a formal blessing.
11tn (27:5) The disjunctive clause (introduced by a conjunction with the subject, followed by the predicate) here introduces a new scene in the story.
12tc (27:5) The LXX adds here "to his father," which may have been accidentally omitted in the MT.
13tn (27:7) Following the imperative, the cohortative (with the prefixed conjunction) indicates purpose or result.
14tn (27:7) The cohortative, with the prefixed conjunction, also expresses logical sequence. See vv. 4, 19, 27.
15tn (27:7) In her report to Jacob, Rebekah plays down Isaac's strong desire to bless Esau by leaving out yv!p=n~ (n~pc!, "my soul"), but by adding the phrase "in the presence of the LORD," she stresses how serious this matter is.
16tn (27:8) Heb "listen to my voice." The Hebrew idiom means "to comply; to obey."
17tn (27:8) Heb "to that which I am commanding you."
18tn (27:9) Following the imperative, the cohortative (with the prefixed conjunction) indicates purpose or result.
19tn (27:10) The form is the perfect tense with the vav consecutive. It carries forward the tone of instruction initiated by the command to "go...and get" in the preceding verse.
20tn (27:10) The form is the perfect with the vav consecutive; it carries the future nuance of the preceding verbs of instruction, but by switching the subject to Jacob, indicates the expected result of the subterfuge.
21tn (27:10) Heb "so that." The conjunction indicates purpose or result.
22tn (27:11) Heb "And Jacob said to Rebekah his mother, `Look, Esau my brother is a hairy man, but I am a smooth [skinned] man.'" The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
23tn (27:12) Heb "Perhaps my father will feel me and I will be in his eyes like a mocker." The Hebrew expression "I will be in his eyes like" means "I would appear to him as."
24tn (27:13) Heb "upon me your curse."
25tn (27:13) Heb "only listen to my voice."
26tn (27:14) The words "the goats" are not in the Hebrew text, but are supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
27tn (27:14) Heb "his mother." This has been replaced by the pronoun "she" in the translation for stylistic reasons.
28tn (27:16) In the Hebrew text the object ("the skins of the young goats") precedes the verb. The disjunctive clause draws attention to this key element in the subterfuge.
29tn (27:16) The word "hands" probably includes the forearms here. How the skins were attached is not specified in the Hebrew text; cf. NLT "she made him a pair of gloves."
30tn (27:17) Heb "gave...into the hand of."
31tn (27:18) Heb "and he said"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
32sn (27:18) Which are you, my son? Isaac's first question shows that the deception is going to require more subterfuge than Rebekah had anticipated. Jacob will have to pull off the deceit.
33tn (27:19) Heb "get up and sit." This may mean simply "sit up," or it may indicate that he was to get up from his couch and sit at a table.
34tn (27:19) Heb "so that your soul may bless me." These words, though not reported by Rebekah to Jacob (see v. 7) accurately reflect what Isaac actually said to Esau (see v. 4). Perhaps Jacob knew more than Rebekah realized, but it is more likely that this was an idiom for sincere blessing with which Jacob was familiar. At any rate, his use of the precise wording was a nice, convincing touch.
35tn (27:20) Heb "What is this?" The enclitic pronoun "this" adds emphasis to the question, which is comparable to the English rhetorical question, "How in the world?"
36tn (27:20) Heb "you hastened to find." In translation the infinitive becomes the main verb and the first verb becomes adverbial.
37tn (27:20) Heb "caused to meet before me."
38tn (27:20) Heb "and he said, `Because the LORD your God....'" The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
39tn (27:21) Following the imperative, the cohortative (with prefixed conjunction) indicates purpose or result.
40tn (27:21) Heb "Are you this one, Esau, my son, or not?" On the use of the interrogative particle here, see BDB 210.
41tn (27:23) Heb "and he blessed him." The referents of the pronouns "he" (Isaac) and "him" (Jacob) have been specified in the translation for clarity.
42tn (27:24) Heb "he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
43tn (27:25) Heb "and he said"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
44tn (27:25) Heb "Bring near to me and I will eat of the wild game, my son." Following the imperative, the cohortative with the prefixed conjunction indicates purpose or result.
45tn (27:25) Heb "so that my soul may bless you." The presence of yv!p=n~ (n~pc!, "my soul") as subject emphasizes Isaac's heartfelt desire to do this. The conjunction indicates that the ritual meal must be first eaten before the formal blessing may be given.
46tn (27:25) Heb "and he brought"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
47tn (27:25) Heb "and he drank"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
48tn (27:27) Heb "and he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
49tn (27:27) Heb "and he smelled the smell"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
50tn (27:27) Heb "see."
51tn (27:28) Heb "and from the dew of the sky."
52tn (27:28) Heb "and from the fatness."
53tn (27:29) Heb "and be." The verb is an imperative, which is used rhetorically in this oracle of blessing. It is an invitation to exercise authority his brothers and indicates that he is granted such authority by the patriarch of the family. Furthermore, the blessing enables the recipient to accomplish this.
54tn (27:29) The Hebrew word is ryb!g+ (g+b!r, "lord; mighty one"). The one being blessed will be stronger and therefore more powerful than his brother. See Gen 25:23. The feminine form of this rare noun means "mistress" or "queen-mother."
55tn (27:29) Following the imperative, the prefixed verbal form (which is either an imperfect or a jussive) with the prefixed conjunction indicates purpose or result.
56tn (27:30) The use of the infinitive absolute before the finite form of the verb makes the construction emphatic.
57tn (27:30) Heb "the presence of Isaac his father." The repetition of the proper name ("Isaac") was
58tn (27:30) Heb "and Esau his brother came from his hunt."
59tn (27:31) Heb "and he said to his father"; the referent of "he" (Esau) has been specified in the translation for clarity, while the words "his father" have been replaced by the pronoun "him" for stylistic reasons.
60tn (27:31) Or "arise" (i.e., sit up).
61tn (27:31) Heb "so that your soul may bless me."
62tn (27:32) Heb "said."
63tn (27:32) Heb "and he said, `I [am] your son, your firstborn.'" The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged for stylistic reasons.
64tn (27:33) Heb "and Isaac trembled with a great trembling to excess." The verb "trembled" is joined with a cognate accusative, which is modified by an adjective "great," and a prepositional phrase "to excess." All of this is emphatic, showing the violence of Isaac's reaction to the news.
65tn (27:33) Heb "Who then is he who hunted game and brought [it] to me so that I ate from all before you arrived and blessed him?"
66tn (27:34) The temporal clause is introduced with the temporal indicator and has the infinitive as its verb.
67tn (27:34) Heb "and he yelled [with] a great and bitter yell to excess."
68tn (27:35) Heb "and he said"; the referent (Isaac) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
69tn (27:35) Or "took"; "received."
70tn (27:36) Heb "Is he not rightly named Jacob?" The rhetorical question, since it expects a positive reply, has been translated as a declarative statement.
71sn (27:36) He has tripped me up. When originally given, the name Jacob was a play on the word "heel" (see Gen 25:26). The name (since it is a verb) probably means something like "may he protect," that is, as a rearguard, dogging the heels. This name was probably chosen because of the immediate association with the incident of grabbing the heel. Esau gives the name "Jacob" a negative connotation here, the meaning "to trip up; to supplant."
72tn (27:38) Heb "Bless me, me also, my father." The words "my father" have not been repeated in the translation for stylistic reasons.
73tn (27:38) Heb "and Esau lifted his voice and wept."
74tn (27:39) Heb "look."
75tn (27:39) Heb "from the fatness."
76sn (27:40) You will tear off his yoke from your neck. It may be that this prophetic blessing found its fulfillment when Jerusalem fell and Edom got its revenge. The oracle makes Edom subservient to Israel and suggests the Edomites would live away from the best land and be forced to sustain themselves by violent measures.
77tn (27:41) Or "bore a grudge against." The Hebrew verb <f^c* (c*f^<) describes persistent hatred.
78tn (27:41) Heb "because of the blessing which his father blessed him."
79tn (27:41) Heb "said in his heart." The expression may mean "said to himself." Even if this is the case, v. 42 makes it clear that he must have shared his intentions with someone, because the news reached Rebekah.
80tn (27:41) Heb "days."
81tn (27:41) The cohortative here expresses Esau's determined resolve to kill Jacob.
82tn (27:42) Heb "and the words of Esau her older son were told to Rebekah."
83tn (27:42) Heb "she sent and called for."
84tn (27:42) Heb "is consoling himself with respect to you to kill you." The only way Esau had of dealing with his anger at the moment was to plan to kill his brother after the death of Isaac.
85tn (27:43) Heb "listen to my voice."
86tn (27:43) Heb "arise, flee."
87tn (27:44) Heb "a few days." Rebekah probably downplays the length of time Jacob will be gone, perhaps to encourage him and assure him that things will settle down soon. She probably expects Esau's anger to die down quickly. However, Jacob ends up being gone twenty years and he never sees Rebekah again.
88tn (27:45) The words "stay there" are supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
89tn (27:45) Heb "and I will send and I will take you from there." The verb "send" has no object in the Hebrew text; one must be supplied in the translation. Either "someone" or "a message" could be supplied, but since in those times a message would require a messenger, "someone" has been used.
90tn (27:45) If Jacob stayed, he would be killed and Esau would be forced to run away.
91tn (27:46) Heb "loathe my life." The Hebrew verb translated "loathe" refers to strong disgust (see Lev 20:23).
92tn (27:46) Some translate the Hebrew term "Heth" as "Hittites" here (see also Gen 23:3), but this gives the impression that these people were the classical Hittites of Anatolia. However, there is no known connection between these sons of Heth, apparently a Canaanite group (see Gen 10:15), and the Hittites of Asia Minor. See H. A. Hoffner, Jr., "Hittites," in Peoples of the Old Testament World (ed. A. J. Hoerth, G. L. Mattingly, and E. M. Yamauchi; Grand Rapids: Baker, 1996) 152-53.
93tn (27:46) Heb "If Jacob takes a wife from the daughters of Heth, like these, from the daughters of the land, why to me life?"
1tn (28:1) Heb "you must not take a wife from the daughters of Canaan."
2tn (28:2) Heb "Arise! Go!" The first of the two imperatives is adverbial and stresses the immediacy of the departure.
3tn (28:3) Heb "El Shaddai." See the extended note on the phrase "Sovereign God" in Gen 17:1.
4tn (28:3) Heb "and make you fruitful and multiply you." See Gen 17:6, 20 for similar terminology.
5tn (28:3) The perfect verbal form with vav consecutive here indicates consequence. The collocation l= + hyh means "become."
6tn (28:3) Heb "an assembly of peoples."
7tn (28:4) Heb "and may he give to you the blessing of Abraham, to you and to your offspring with you." The name "Abraham" is an objective genitive here; this refers to the blessing that God gave to Abraham.
8tn (28:4) The words "the land" have been supplied in the translation for clarity.
9tn (28:4) Heb "the land of your sojournings," that is, the land where Jacob had been living as a resident alien, as his future descendants would after him.
10tn (28:6) Heb "to take for himself from there a wife."
11tn (28:6) The infinitive construct with the preposition and the suffix form a temporal clause.
12tn (28:6) Heb "you must not take a wife from the daughters of Canaan."
13tn (28:8) Heb "saw."
14tn (28:8) Heb "the daughters of Canaan."
15tn (28:8) Heb "evil in the eyes of."
16tn (28:9) Heb "took for a wife."
17tn (28:11) Heb "the place." The article may indicate simply that the place is definite in the mind of the narrator. However, as the story unfolds the place is transformed into a holy place. See A. P. Ross, "Jacob's Vision: The Founding of Bethel," BSac 142 (1985): 224-37.
18tn (28:11) Heb "and he spent the night there because the sun had gone down."
19tn (28:11) Heb "he took from the stones of the place," which here means Jacob took one of the stones (see v. 18).
20tn (28:11) Heb "and he put [it at] the place of his head." The text does not actually say the stone was placed under his head to serve as a pillow, though most interpreters and translators assume this. Perhaps the stone served some other purpose. Possibly Jacob, who appears to have had a primitive religious mindset, regarded it as a good luck charm or as a magical stone in which a protective spirit resided. Note that later in the story he anticipates the stone becoming the residence of God (see v. 22). Many primitive cultures throughout the world view certain types of stones as magical and/or sacred. See J. G. Frazer, Folklore in the Old Testament (rpt; New York: Hart, 1975) 231-37.
21tn (28:11) Heb "lay down."
22tn (28:12) Heb "and dreamed."
23tn (28:12) Heb "and look." The scene which Jacob witnessed is described in three clauses introduced with hN}h! (h!N@h). In this way the narrator invites the reader to witness the scene through Jacob's eyes. Fokkelman points out that the particle goes with a lifted arm and an open mouth: "There, a ladder! Oh, angels! and look, the LORD himself" (J. P. Fokkelman, Narrative Art in Genesis, 51-52).
24tn (28:12) The Hebrew noun <L*s% (s%L*<, "ladder," "stairway") occurs only here in the OT, but there appears to be an Akkadian cognate simmiltu (with metathesis of the second and third consonants and a feminine ending) which has a specialized meaning of "stairway, ramp." See H. R. Cohen, Biblical Hapax Legomena in the Light of Akkadian and Ugaritic, 34. For further discussion see C. Houtman, "What Did Jacob See in His Dream at Bethel? Some Remarks on Genesis 28:10-22," VT 27 (1977): 337-52; J. G. Griffiths, "The Celestial Ladder and the Gate of Heaven," ExpTim 76 (1964/65): 229-30; and A. R. Millard, "The Celestial Ladder and the Gate of Heaven," ExpTim 78 (1966/67): 86-87.
25tn (28:13) Heb "the God of your father Abraham and the God of Isaac." The Hebrew word for "father" can typically be used in a broader sense than the English word, in this case referring to Abraham (who was Jacob's grandfather). For stylistic reasons and for clarity, the words "your father" are supplied with "Isaac" in the translation.
26tn (28:13) The Hebrew term Jr\a# (a#r#J) can mean "[the] earth," "land," "region," "piece of ground," or "ground" depending on the context. Here the term specifically refers to the plot of ground on which Jacob was lying, but at the same time this stands by metonymy for the entire land of Canaan.
27tn (28:14) This is the same Hebrew word translated "ground" in the preceding verse.
28tn (28:14) The verb is singular in the Hebrew; Jacob is addressed as the representative of his descendants.
29tn (28:14) Theoretically the Niphal stem can be translated either as passive or reflexive/reciprocal. (The Niphal of "bless" is only used in formulations of the Abrahamic covenant. See Gen 12:2; 18:18; 28:14.) Traditionally the verb is taken as passive here, as if Jacob were going to be a channel or source of blessing. But in other formulations of the Abrahamic covenant (see Gen 22:18; 26:4) the Hitpael replaces this Niphal form, suggesting a translation "will bless (i.e., pronounce blessings upon) themselves/one another." The Hitpael of "bless" is used with a reflexive/reciprocal sense in Deut 29:18; Ps 72:17; Isa 65:16; Jer 4:2. Gen 28:14 predicts that Jacob will be held up as a paradigm of divine blessing and that people will use his name in their blessing formulae (see Gen 12:2 and 18:18 as well, where Abram/Abraham receives this promise). For examples of blessing formulae utilizing an individual as an example of blessing see Gen 48:20 and Ruth 4:11.
30tn (28:14) Heb "and they will pronounce blessings by you, all the families of the earth, and by your offspring."
31tn (28:15) Heb "Look, I [am] with you." The clause is a nominal clause; the verb to be supplied could be present (as in the translation) or future, "Look, I [will be] with you" (cf. NEB).
32tn (28:16) Heb "woke up from his sleep." This has been simplified in the translation for stylistic reasons.
33tn (28:16) Heb "said."
34tn (28:18) Heb "and he got up early...and he took."
35tn (28:18) Heb "he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
36tn (28:18) See the note on this phrase in v. 11.
37tn (28:18) Heb "standing stone."
sn (28:18) Sacred stone. Such a stone could be used as a boundary marker, a burial stone, or as a shrine. Here the stone is intended to be a reminder of the stairway that was "erected" and on which the LORD "stood." (In Hebrew the word translated "sacred stone" is derived from the verb translated "erected" in v. 12 and "stood" in v. 13. Since the top of the stairway reached the heavens where the LORD stood, Jacob poured oil on the top of the stone. See C. F. Graesser, "Standing Stones in Ancient Palestine," BA 35 (1972): 34-63; and E. Stockton, "Sacred Pillars in the Bible," ABR 20 (1972): 16-32.
38tn (28:19) The name Bethel means "house of God" in Hebrew (see v. 17).
39tn (28:20) Heb "bread," although the term can be used for food in general.
40tn (28:21) Heb "and I return in peace to the house of my father."
41tn (28:22) The disjunctive clause structure (conjunction + noun/subject) is used to highlight the statement.
42tn (28:22) The infinitive absolute is used before the finite verb for emphasis.
43tn (28:22) Heb "and all which you give to me I will surely give a tenth of it to you." The disjunctive clause structure (conjunction + noun/object) highlights this statement as well.
1tn (29:1) Heb "and Jacob lifted up his feet." This unusual expression suggests that Jacob had a new lease on life now that God had promised him the blessing he had so desperately tried to gain by his own efforts. The text portrays him as having a new step in his walk.
2tn (29:1) Heb "the land of the sons of the east."
3tn (29:2) Heb "and he saw, and look." As in Gen 28:12-15, the narrator uses the particle hN}h! (h!N@h, "look") here and in the next clause to draw the reader into the story.
4tn (29:2) Heb "and look, there."
5tn (29:2) The disjunctive clause (introduced by the noun with the prefixed conjunction) provides supplemental information that is important to the story.
6tn (29:3) Heb "they"; the referent (the shepherds) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
7tn (29:5) Heb "son."
8tn (29:5) Heb "and they said, `We know.'" The word "him" is not in the Hebrew text, but has been supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons. In the translation several introductory clauses throughout this section have been placed after the direct discourse they introduce for stylistic reasons as well.
9tn (29:6) Heb "and he said to them, `Is there peace to him?'"
10tn (29:6) Heb "peace."
11tn (29:7) Heb "and he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
12tn (29:7) Heb "the day is great."
13tn (29:7) Heb "water the sheep and go and pasture [them]." The verbal forms are imperatives, but Jacob would hardly be giving direct orders to someone else's shepherds. The nuance here is probably one of advice.
14tn (29:8) The perfect verbal forms with the vav consecutive carry on the sequence begun by the initial imperfect form.
15tn (29:9) Heb "was a shepherdess."
16tn (29:10) Heb "Laban, the brother of his mother" (twice in this verse).
17tn (29:10) Heb "Jacob." The proper name has been replaced by the pronoun ("he") in the translation for stylistic reasons.
18tn (29:10) Heb "drew near, approached."
19tn (29:10) Heb "Laban, the brother of his mother." The text says nothing initially about the beauty of Rachel. But the reader is struck by the repetition of "Laban the brother of his mother." Wenham is no doubt correct when he observes that Jacob's primary motive at this stage is to ingratiate himself with Laban (G. Wenham, Genesis 16-50, 231).
20tn (29:11) Heb "and he lifted up his voice and wept." The idiom calls deliberate attention to the fact that Jacob wept out loud.
21tn (29:12) Heb "declared."
22tn (29:12) Heb "that he [was] the brother of her father."
23tn (29:13) Heb "he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
24tn (29:13) Heb "and he told to Laban all these things." This might mean Jacob told Laban how he happened to be there, but Laban's response (see v. 14) suggests "all these things" refers to what Jacob had previously told Rachel (see v. 12).
25tn (29:14) Heb "indeed, my bone and my flesh are you." The expression sounds warm enough, but the presence of "indeed" may suggest that Laban had to be convinced of Jacob's identity before permitting him to stay. To be one's "bone and flesh" is to be someone's blood relative. For example, the phrase describes the relationship between Abimelech and the Shechemites (Judg 9:2; his mother was a Shechemite); David and the Israelites (2 Sam 5:1); David and the elders of Judah (2 Sam 19:12,); and David and his nephew Amasa (2 Sam 19:13, see 2 Sam 17:2; 1 Chr 2:16-17).
26tn (29:14) Heb "and he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
27tn (29:14) Heb "a month of days."
28tn (29:15) The verb is the perfect with the vav consecutive; the nuance in the question is deliberative.
29tn (29:15) Heb "my brother." The term "brother" is used in a loose sense; actually Jacob was Laban's nephew.
30tn (29:16) Heb "and to Laban [there were] two daughters." The disjunctive clause (introduced here by a conjunction and a prepositional phrase) provides supplemental material that is important to the story. Since this material is parenthetical in nature, vv. 16-17 have been set in parentheses in the translation.
31tn (29:17) Heb "and the eyes of Leah were tender." The disjunctive clause (introduced here by a conjunction and a noun) continues the parenthesis begun in v. 16. It is not clear what is meant by "tender" (or "delicate") eyes. The expression may mean she had appealing eyes (cf. NAB, NRSV, NLT), though some suggest that they were plain, not having the brightness normally expected. Either way, she did not measure up to her gorgeous sister.
32tn (29:17) Heb "and Rachel was beautiful of form and beautiful of appearance."
33tn (29:18) Heb "Jacob loved."
34tn (29:19) Heb "Better my giving her to you than my giving her to another man."
35tn (29:20) Heb "in exchange for Rachel."
36sn (29:20) But they seemed like only a few days to him. This need not mean that the time passed quickly. More likely it means that the price seemed insignificant when compared to what he was getting in the bargain.
37tn (29:20) Heb "because of his love for her." The words "was so great" are supplied for stylistic reasons.
38tn (29:21) Heb "and Jacob said."
39tn (29:21) Heb "my days are fulfilled."
40tn (29:21) Heb "and I will go in to her." The verb is a cohortative; it may be subordinated to the preceding request, "that I may go in," or it may be an independent clause expressing his desire. The verb "go in" in this context refers to sexual intercourse (i.e., the consummation of the marriage).
41tn (29:22) Heb "men."
42tn (29:23) Heb "and it happened in the evening that he took Leah his daughter and brought her."
sn (29:23) His daughter Leah. Laban's deception of Jacob by giving him the older daughter instead of the younger was God's way of disciplining the deceiver who tricked his older brother. Kidner says this account is "the very embodiment of anti-climax, and this moment a miniature of man's disillusion, experienced from Eden onwards" (D. Kidner, Genesis, 160). Von Rad notes, "That Laban secretly gave the unloved Leah to the man in love was, to be sure, a monstrous blow, a masterpiece of shameless treachery...It was certainly a move by which he won for himself far and wide the coarsest laughter" (G. von Rad, Genesis, 291).
43tn (29:23) Heb "to him"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
44tn (29:23) Heb "he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
45tn (29:23) Heb "went in to her." The expression "went in to" in this context refers to sexual intercourse, i.e., the consummation of the marriage.
46tn (29:24) Heb "and Laban gave to her Zilpah his female servant, to Leah his daughter [for] a servant." This clause gives information parenthetical to the narrative.
47tn (29:25) Heb "and it happened in the morning that look, it was Leah." By the use of the particle hN}h! (h!N@h, "look"), the narrator invites the reader to view the scene through Jacob's eyes.
48tn (29:25) Heb "and he said"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
49tn (29:25) Heb What is this you have done to me?" The use of the pronoun "this" is enclitic, adding emphasis to the question: "What in the world have you done to me?"
50sn (29:25) The Hebrew verb translated tricked here (r*m*h [hmr]) is cognate to the noun used in Gen 27:35 to describe Jacob's deception of Esau. Jacob is discovering that what goes around, comes around! See J. A. Diamond, "The Deception of Jacob: A New Perspective on an Ancient Solution to the Problem," VT 34 (1984): 211-13.
51tn (29:26) Heb "and Laban said, `It is not done so in our place.'" The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
52tn (29:26) Heb "to give the younger." The words "daughter" and "in marriage" are supplied in the translation for clarity and for stylistic reasons.
53tn (29:27) Heb "fulfill the period of seven of this one." The referent of "this one" has been specified in the translation as "my older daughter" for clarity.
sn (29:27) Bridal week. An ancient Hebrew marriage ceremony included an entire week of festivities (cf. Judg 14:12).
54tn (29:27) Heb "this other one."
55tn (29:27) Heb "and we will give to you also this one in exchange for labor which you will work with me, still seven other years."
sn (29:27) In exchange for seven more years of work. See C. H. Gordon, "The Story of Jacob and Laban in the Light of the Nuzi Tablets," BASOR 66 (1937): 25-27; and J. Van Seters, "Jacob's Marriages and Ancient Near Eastern Customs: A Reassessment," HTR 62 (1969): 377-95.
56tn (29:28) Heb "and Jacob did so." The words "as Laban said" are supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
57tn (29:28) Heb "and he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
58tn (29:28) Heb "the seven of this one." The referent of "this one" has been specified in the translation as Leah to avoid confusion with Rachel, mentioned later in the verse.
59tn (29:28) Heb "and he gave to him Rachel his daughter for him for a wife." The referent of the pronoun "he" (Laban) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
60tn (29:29) Heb "and Laban gave to Rachel his daughter Bilhah his female servant, for her for a servant."
61tn (29:30) Heb "and he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
62tn (29:30) Heb "went in also to Rachel." The expression "went in to" in this context refers to sexual intercourse, i.e., the consummation of the marriage.
63tn (29:30) Heb "him"; the referent (Laban) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
64tn (29:30) Heb "and he loved also Rachel, more than Leah, and he served with him still seven other years."
65tn (29:31) Heb "hated." The rhetorical device of overstatement is used (note v. 30, which says simply that Jacob loved Rachel more than he did Leah) to emphasize that Rachel, as Jacob's true love and the primary object of his affections, had an advantage over Leah.
66tn (29:31) Heb "he opened up her womb."
67tn (29:32) Or "Leah conceived" (also in vv. 33, 34, 35).
68sn (29:32) The name Reuben means "look, a son."
69tn (29:32) Heb "looked on my affliction."
sn (29:32) Leah's explanation of the name Reuben reflects a popular etymology, not an exact one. The name means literally "look, a son." Playing on the Hebrew verb "look," she observes that the LORD has "looked" with pity on her oppressed condition. See further S. R. Driver, Book of Genesis, 273.
70tn (29:33) Heb "hated." See the note on the word "unloved" in v. 31.
71sn (29:33) The name Simeon is derived from the verbal root c*m*u (umv) and means "hearing." The name is appropriate since it is reminder that the LORD "heard" about Leah's unloved condition and responded with pity.
72tn (29:34) Heb "will be joined to me."
73sn (29:34) The name Levi (the precise meaning of which is debated) was appropriate because it sounds like the verb l*w´h (hwl), "to join," used in the statement recorded earlier in the verse.
74sn (29:35) The name Judah means "he will be praised" and reflects the sentiment Leah expresses in the statement recorded earlier in the verse. For further discussion see W. F. Albright, "The Names `Israel' and `Judah' with an Excursis on the Etymology of Todah and Torah," JBL 46 (1927): 151-85; and A. R. Millard, "The Meaning of the Name Judah," ZAW 86 (1974): 216-18.
1tn (30:1) Heb "Rachel." The proper name has been replaced by the pronoun ("she") in the translation for stylistic reasons.
2tn (30:1) Heb "sons."
3tn (30:2) Heb "and the anger of Jacob was hot."
4tn (30:2) Heb "who has withheld from you the fruit of the womb."
5tn (30:3) Heb "go in to." The expression "go in to" in this context refers to sexual intercourse.
6tn (30:3) After the imperative, the prefixed verbal form with the conjunction indicates the immediate purpose of the proposed activity.
7tn (30:3) The word "children" is not in the Hebrew text but has been supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
8tn (30:3) Heb "upon my knees." This is an idiomatic way of saying that Bilhah will be simply a surrogate mother. Rachel will adopt the child as her own.
9tn (30:3) Heb "and I will be built up, even I, from her." The prefixed verbal form with the conjunction is subordinated to the preceding prefixed verbal form and gives the ultimate purpose for the proposed action. The idiom of "built up" here refers to having a family (see Gen 16:2, as well as Ruth 4:11 and BDB 125).
10tn (30:4) Heb "and she"; the referent (Rachel) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
11tn (30:4) Heb "went in to." The expression "went in to" in this context refers to sexual intercourse.
12tn (30:5) Or "Bilhah conceived" (also in v. 7).
13tn (30:5) Heb "and she bore for Jacob a son."
14tn (30:6) Heb "and also he has heard my voice." The expression means that God responded positively to Rachel's cry and granted her request.
15tn (30:6) Or "therefore."
16sn (30:6) The name Dan means "he vindicated" or "he judged." The name plays on the verb used in the statement which appears earlier in the verse. The verb translated "vindicated" is from D!/ (/yD]), "to judge; to vindicate," the same verbal root from which the name is derived. Rachel sensed that God was righting the wrong.
17tn (30:7) Heb "and she became pregnant again and Bilhah, the servant of Rachel, bore a second son for Jacob."
18tn (30:8) Heb "[with] a mighty struggle I have struggled with my sister, also I have prevailed." The phrase "mighty struggle" reads literally "struggles of God." The plural participle "struggles" reflects the ongoing nature of the struggle, while the divine name is used here idiomatically to emphasize the intensity of the struggle. See Skinner, Genesis, 387.
19sn (30:8) The name Naphtali must mean something like "my struggle" in view of the statement Rachel made in the preceding clause. The name plays on this earlier statement, "[with] a mighty struggle I have struggled with my sister."
20tn (30:9) Heb "she took her servant Zilpah and gave her." The verbs "took" and "gave" are treated as a hendiadys in the translation: "she gave."
21tn (30:10) Heb "and Zilpah, the servant of Leah, bore for Jacob a son."
22tc (30:11) The statement in the Kethib (consonantal text) appears to mean literally "with good fortune," if one takes the initial bet as a preposition indicating accompaniment. The Qere (marginal reading) means "good fortune has arrived."
23sn (30:11) The name Gad means "good fortune." The name reflects Leah's feeling that good fortune has come her way, as expressed in her statement recorded earlier in the verse.
24tn (30:12) Heb "and Zilpah, the servant of Leah, bore a second son for Jacob."
25tn (30:13) The Hebrew statement apparently means "with my happiness."
26tn (30:13) Heb "daughters."
27sn (30:13) The name Asher apparently means "happy one." The name plays on the words used in the statement which appears earlier in the verse. Both the Hebrew noun and verb translated "happy" and "call me happy," respectively, are derived from the same root as the name Asher.
28tn (30:14) Heb "during the days."
29sn (30:14) Mandrake plants were popularly believed to be an aphrodisiac in the culture of the time.
30tn (30:15) Heb "and she said to her"; the referent of the pronoun "she" (Leah) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
31tn (30:15) Heb "therefore."
32tn (30:15) Heb "lie down." The expression "lie down with" in this context (here and in the following verse) refers to sexual intercourse. The imperfect verbal form has a permissive nuance here.
33tn (30:16) Heb "must come in to me." The imperfect verbal form has an obligatory nuance here. She has acquired him for the night and feels he is obligated to have sexual relations with her.
34tn (30:16) Heb "I have surely hired." The infinitive absolute precedes the finite verbal form for emphasis. The name Issachar (see v. 18) seems to be related to this expression.
35tn (30:16) This is the same Hebrew verb (bk^v* [v*k^b]) translated "sleep with" in v. 15. In direct discourse the more euphemistic "sleep with" was used, but here in the narrative "marital relations" reflects more clearly the emphasis on sexual intercourse.
36tn (30:17) Heb "listened to."
37tn (30:17) Or "she conceived" (also in v. 19).
38tn (30:17) Heb "and she bore for Jacob a fifth son," i.e., this was the fifth son that Leah had given Jacob.
39tn (30:18) Heb "God has given my reward."
40tn (30:18) The words "as a wife" are not in the Hebrew text, but are supplied for clarity (cf. v. 9).
sn (30:18) Leah seems to regard the act of giving her servant Zilpah to her husband as a sacrifice, for which (she believes) God is now rewarding her with the birth of a son.
41sn (30:18) The name Issachar appears to mean "man of reward" or possibly "there is reward." The name plays on the word used in the statement made earlier in the verse. The Hebrew noun translated "reward" is derived from the same root as the name Issachar. The irony is that Rachel thought the mandrakes would work for her, and she was willing to trade one night for them. But in that one night Leah became pregnant.
42tn (30:19) Heb "and she bore a sixth son for Jacob," i.e., this was the sixth son that Leah had given Jacob.
43sn (30:20) The name Zebulun apparently means "honor." The name plays on the verb used in the statement made earlier in the verse. The Hebrew verb translated "will honor" and the name Zebulun derive from the same root.
44tn (30:22) Heb "remembered."
45tn (30:22) Heb "and God listened to her and opened up her womb." Since "God" is the subject of the previous clause, the noun has been replaced by the pronoun "he" in the translation for stylistic reasons
46tn (30:23) Or "conceived."
47tn (30:23) Heb "my reproach." A "reproach" is a cutting taunt or painful ridicule, but here it probably refers by metonymy to Rachel's barren condition, which was considered shameful in this culture and was the reason why she was the object of taunting and ridicule.
48sn (30:24) The name Joseph means "may he add." The name expresses Rachel's desire to have an additional son. In Hebrew the name sounds like the verb a*s*[ ([sa), translated "taken away" in the earlier statement made in v. 23. So the name, while reflecting Rachel's hope, was also a reminder that God had removed her shame.
49tn (30:25) The perfect verbal form is translated as a past perfect because Rachel's giving birth to Joseph preceded Jacob's conversation with Laban.
50tn (30:25) The imperatival form here expresses a request.
sn (30:25) For Jacob to ask to leave would mean that seven more years had passed. Thus all Jacob's children were born within the range of seven years of each other, with Joseph coming right at the end of the seven years.
51tn (30:25) Following the imperative, the cohortative with the prefixed conjunction indicates purpose or result.
52tn (30:25) Heb "to my place and to my land."
53tn (30:26) Heb "give my wives and my children, for whom I have served you." In one sense Laban had already "given" Jacob his two daughters as wives (Gen 29:21, 28). Here Jacob was asking for permission to take his own family along with him on the journey back to Canaan.
54tn (30:26) Following the imperative, the cohortative with the prefixed conjunction indicates purpose or result.
55tn (30:26) Heb "for you, you know my service [with] which I have served you."
56tn (30:27) The words "please stay here" have been supplied in the translation for clarification and for stylistic reasons.
57tn (30:27) Or perhaps "I have grown rich and the LORD has blessed me" (cf. NEB). See J. Finkelstein, JAOS 88 (1968) 34, n. 19.
58tn (30:28) Heb "set your wage for me so I may give [it]."
59tn (30:29) Heb "and he said to him, `You know how I have served you.'" The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons, and the referent of the pronoun "he" (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
60tn (30:29) Heb "and how your cattle were with me."
61tn (30:30) Or "for."
62tn (30:30) Heb "before me."
63tn (30:30) Heb "and it has broken out with respect to abundance."
64tn (30:30) Heb "at my foot."
65tn (30:30) Heb "How long [until] I do, also I, for my house?"
66tn (30:31) Heb "and he said." The referent (Laban) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
67tn (30:31) The negated imperfect verbal form has an obligatory nuance.
68tn (30:31) The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
69tn (30:31) Heb "If you do for me this thing."
70tn (30:31) Heb "I will return, I will tend," an idiom meaning "I will continue tending."
71tn (30:32) Heb "pass through."
72tn (30:32) Or "every black lamb"; Heb "and every dark sheep among the lambs."
73tn (30:32) Heb "and the spotted and speckled among the goats."
74tn (30:32) Heb "and it will be my wage." The referent collective singular pronoun ("it) has been specified as "these animals" in the translation for clarity.
75tn (30:33) Heb "will answer on my behalf."
76tn (30:33) Heb "on the following day," or "tomorrow."
77tn (30:33) Heb "when you come concerning my wage before you."
sn (30:33) Only the wage we agreed on. Jacob would have to be considered completely honest here, for he would have no control over the kind of animals born; and there could be no disagreement over which animals were his wages.
78tn (30:33) Heb "every one which is not speckled and spotted among the lambs and dark among the goats, stolen it is with me."
79tn (30:34) Heb "and Laban said, `Good, let it be according to your word.'" On the asseverative use of the particle Wl (lW) here, see HALOT 521.
80tn (30:35) Heb "he"; the referent (Laban) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
81tn (30:35) Heb "and he gave [them] into the hand."
82tn (30:36) Heb "and he put a journey of three days between himself and Jacob."
sn (30:36) Three days' traveling distance from Jacob. Speiser observes, "Laban is delighted with the terms, and promptly proceeds to violate the spirit of the bargain by removing to a safe distance all the grown animals that would be likely to produce the specified sports" (E. A. Speiser, Genesis, 238). Laban apparently thought that by separating out the spotted, striped, and dark colored animals he could minimize the production of spotted, striped, or dark offspring that would then belong to Jacob.
83tn (30:36) The disjunctive clause (introduced by the vav with subject) is circumstantial/temporal; Laban removed the animals while Jacob was taking care of the rest.
84sn (30:38) He put the branches in front of the flocks...when they came to drink. It was generally believed that placing such "visual aids" before the animals as they were mating, it was possible to influence the appearance of their offspring. Speiser notes that "Jacob finds a way to outwit his father-in-law, through prenatal conditioning of the flock by visual aids--in conformance with universal folk beliefs" (E. A. Speiser, Genesis, 238). Nevertheless, in spite of Jacob's efforts at animal husbandry, he still attributes the resulting success to God (see 31:5).
85tn (30:39) The Hebrew verb used here can mean "to be in heat" (see v. 38) or "to mate; to conceive; to become pregnant." The latter nuance makes better sense in this verse, for the next clause describes them giving birth.
86tn (30:39) Heb "the sheep." The noun has been replaced by the pronoun ("they") in the translation for stylistic reasons.
87tn (30:40) Heb "and he set the faces of."
88tn (30:41) Heb "and at every breeding-heat of the flock."
89tn (30:42) Heb "he did not put [them] in." The referent of the [understood] direct object, "them," has been specified as "the branches" in the translation for clarity.
90tn (30:42) Heb "were for Laban."
91tn (30:43) Heb "the man"; Jacob's name has been supplied in the translation for clarity.
92tn (30:43) Heb "and there were to him."
1tn (31:1) Heb "and he heard the words of the sons of Laban, saying."
2sn (31:1) The Hebrew word translated "gotten rich" (K*b{d [dobK*], "wealth") has the basic idea of "weight." If one is heavy with possessions, then that one is wealthy (13:2). Abraham, Jacob, and Joseph all become wealthy when they left the promised land. Jacob's wealth foreshadows what will happen to Israel when they leave the land of Egypt (Exod 12:35-38).
3tn (31:1) Heb "and from that which belonged to our father he has gained all this wealth."
4tn (31:2) Heb "and Jacob saw the face of Laban, and look, he was not with him as formerly." Jacob knew from the expression on Laban's face that his attitude towards him had changed--Jacob had become persona non grata.
5tn (31:3) Or perhaps "ancestors" (so NRSV), although the only "ancestors" Jacob had there were his grandfather Abraham and his father Isaac!
6sn (31:3) I will be with you. Though Laban was no longer "with him," the LORD promised to be.
7tn (31:4) Heb "sent and called for Rachel and for Leah." Jacob did not go in person, but probably sent a servant with a message for his wives to meet him in the field.
8tn (31:4) Heb "the field." The word is an adverbial accusative, indicating that this is where Jacob wanted them to meet him. The words "to come to" are supplied in the translation for clarification and stylistic reasons.
9tn (31:4) Heb "to his flock."
10tn (31:5) Heb "I see the face of your father, that he is not toward me as formerly."
11tn (31:6) Heb "with all my strength."
12tn (31:7) This rare verb means "to make a fool of" someone. It involves deceiving someone so that their public reputation suffers (see Exod 8:25).
13tn (31:8) In the protasis ("if" section) of this conditional clause, the imperfect verbal form has a customary nuance--whatever he would say worked to Jacob's benefit.
14tn (31:8) Heb "speckled" (twice this verse). The word "animals" (after the first occurrence of "speckled") and "offspring" (after the second) have been supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons. The same two terms ("animals" and "offspring") have been supplied after the two occurrences of "streaked" later in this verse.
15tn (31:10) The sentence begins with the temporal indicator, "and it happened at the time of."
16tn (31:10) Heb "in the time of the breeding of the flock I lifted up my eyes and I saw."
17tn (31:10) Heb "going up on," that is, mounting for intercourse.
18tn (31:12) Heb "lift up (now) your eyes and see."
19tn (31:12) Heb "going up on," that is, mounting for intercourse.
20sn (31:13) You anointed the sacred stone. In Gen 28:18 the text simply reported that Jacob poured oil on top of the stone. Now that pouring is interpreted by the LORD as an anointing. Jacob had consecrated the place.
21sn (31:13) And made a vow to me. The second clause reminds Jacob of the vow he made to the LORD when he anointed the stone (Gen 28:20-22). God is now going to take him back to the land, and so he will have to fulfill his vow.
22tn (31:13) Heb "arise, leave!" The first imperative draws attention to the need for immediate action.
sn (31:13) Leave this land immediately. The decision to leave was a wise one in view of the changed attitude in Laban and his sons. But more than that, it was the will of God. Jacob needed to respond to God's call--the circumstances simply made it easier.
23tn (31:14) The two nouns may form a hendiadys, meaning "a share in the inheritance" or "a portion to inherit."
24tn (31:15) Heb "and he devoured, even devouring." The infinitive absolute (following the finite verb here) is used for emphasis.
sn (31:15) He sold us and...wasted our money. The precise nature of Rachel's and Leah's complaint is not entirely clear. Since Jacob had to work to pay for them, they probably mean that their father has cheated Jacob and therefore cheated them as well. See M. Burrows, "The Complaint of Laban's Daughters," JAOS 57 (1937): 250-76.
25tn (31:15) Heb "our money." The word "money" is used figuratively here; it means the price paid for Leah and Rachel. A literal translation ("our money") makes it sound as if Laban wasted money that belonged to Rachel and Leah, rather than the money paid for them.
26tn (31:17) Heb "and Jacob arose and he lifted up his sons and his wives on to the camels."
27tn (31:18) Heb "drove," but this is subject to misunderstanding in contemporary English.
28tn (31:18) Heb "and he led away all his cattle and all his moveable property which he acquired, the cattle he obtained, which he acquired in Paddan Aram to go to Isaac his father to the land of Canaan."
29tn (31:19) This disjunctive clause (note the pattern conjunction + subject + verb) introduces a new scene. In the English translation it may be subordinated to the following clause.
30tn (31:19) Or "household gods." Some translations merely transliterate the Hebrew term <yp!r´T= (T=r*p!m) as "teraphim," which apparently refers to household idols. Some contend that possession of these idols guaranteed the right of inheritance, but it is more likely that they were viewed simply as protective deities. See M. Greenberg, "Another Look at Rachel's Theft of the Teraphim," JBL 81 (1962): 239-48.
31tn (31:20) Heb "stole the heart of," an expression which apparently means "to deceive." The repetition of the verb "to steal" shows that Jacob and Rachel are kindred spirits. Any thought that Laban would have resigned himself to their departure was now out of the question.
32tn (31:20) Heb "fleeing," which reflects Jacob's viewpoint.
33tn (31:21) Heb "and he fled."
34tn (31:21) Heb "he arose and crossed." The first verb emphasizes that he wasted no time in getting across.
35tn (31:21) Heb "the river"; the referent (the Euphrates) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
36tn (31:21) Heb "he set his face."
37tn (31:22) Heb "and it was told to Laban on the third day that Jacob had fled."
38tn (31:23) Heb "his brothers."
39tn (31:23) Heb "him"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
40tn (31:23) Heb "and he pursued after him a journey of seven days."
41tn (31:23) Heb "drew close to."
42tn (31:24) Heb "said to him."
43tn (31:24) Heb "watch yourself," which is a warning to be on guard against doing something that is inappropriate.
44tn (31:24) Heb "lest you speak with Jacob from good to evil." The precise meaning of the expression, which occurs only here and in v. 29, is uncertain. Since Laban proceeded to speak to Jacob at length, it cannot mean to maintain silence. Nor does it seem to be a prohibition against criticism (see vv. 26-30). Most likely it refers to a formal pronouncement, whether it be a blessing or a curse. Laban was to avoid saying anything to Jacob that would be intended to enhance him or to harm him.
45tn (31:25) Heb "and Jacob pitched his tent in the hill country, and Laban pitched with his brothers in the hill country of Gilead." The juxtaposition of disjunctive clauses (note the pattern conjunction + subject + verb in both clauses) indicates synchronism of action.
46tn (31:26) Heb "and you have stolen my heart." This expression apparently means "to deceive" (see v. 20).
47tn (31:26) Heb "and you have led away my daughters like captives of a sword."
48tn (31:27) Heb "Why did you hide in order to flee?" The verb "hide" and the infinitive "to flee" form a hendiadys, the infinitive becoming the main verb and the other the adverb: "flee secretly."
49tn (31:27) Heb "and steal me."
50tn (31:27) Heb "And [why did] you not tell me so I could send you off with joy and with songs, with a tambourine and with a harp?"
51tn (31:28) Heb "my sons and my daughters." Here "sons" refers to "grandsons," and has been translated "grandchildren" since at least one granddaughter, Dinah, was involved. The order has been reversed in the translation for stylistic reasons.
52tn (31:29) Heb "there is to my hand."
53tn (31:29) Heb "watch yourself," which is a warning to be on guard against doing something that is inappropriate.
54tn (31:29) Heb "from speaking with Jacob from good to evil." The precise meaning of the expression, which occurs only here and in v. 24, is uncertain. See the note on the same phrase in v. 24.
55tn (31:30) Heb "and now." The words "I understand that" have been supplied in the translation for clarity and for stylistic reasons.
56tn (31:30) The infinitive absolute appears before the perfect verbal form to emphasize the certainty of the action.
57tn (31:30) The infinitive absolute appears before the perfect verbal form to emphasize the degree of emotion involved.
58sn (31:30) Yet why did you steal my gods? This last sentence is dropped into the speech rather suddenly. See C. Mabee, "Jacob and Laban: The Structure of Judicial Proceedings," VT 30 (1980): 192-207, and G. W. Coats, "Self-Abasement and Insult Formulas," JBL 91 (1972): 90-92.
59tn (31:31) Heb "and Jacob answered and said to Laban, `Because I was afraid.'" This statement is a not a response to the question about Laban's household gods that immediately precedes, but to the earlier question about Jacob's motivation for leaving so quickly and secretly (see v. 27). For this reason the words "I left secretly" are supplied in the translation to indicate the connection to Laban's earlier question in v. 27. Additionally the order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse have been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
60tn (31:31) Heb "for I said."
61tn (31:31) Heb "lest you steal your daughters from with me."
62tn (31:32) Heb "With whomever you find your gods, he will not live."
63tn (31:32) Heb "brothers."
64tn (31:32) Heb "recognize for yourself what is with me and take for yourself."
65tn (31:32) The disjunctive clause (introduced here by a vav conjunction) provides supplemental material that is important to the story. Since this material is parenthetical in nature, it has been placed in parentheses in the translation.
66tn (31:33) No direct object is specified for the verb "find" in the Hebrew text. The words "the idols" have been supplied in the translation for clarification.
67tn (31:33) Heb "and he went out from the tent of Leah and went into the tent of Rachel."
68tn (31:34) The "camel's saddle" was probably some sort of basket-saddle, a cushioned saddle with a basket bound on. Cf. NAB "inside a camel cushion."
69tn (31:34) The disjunctive clause (introduced by a vav conjunction) provides another parenthetical statement necessary to the storyline.
70tn (31:34) The word "them" has been supplied in the translation for clarification.
71tn (31:35) Heb "she"; the referent (Rachel) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
72tn (31:35) Heb "let it not be hot in the eyes of my lord." This idiom refers to anger, in this case as a result of Rachel's failure to stand in the presence of her father as a sign of respect.
73tn (31:35) Heb "I am unable to rise."
74tn (31:35) Heb "the way of women is to me." This idiom refers to a woman's menstrual period.
75tn (31:35) The word "thoroughly" is not in the Hebrew text, but is implied.
76tn (31:36) Heb "it was hot to Jacob." This idiom refers to anger.
77tn (31:36) Heb "and Jacob answered and said to Laban, `What is my sin?'" The proper name "Jacob" has been replaced by the pronoun ("he") in the translation and the order of the introductory clause and direct discourse rearranged for stylistic reasons.
78tn (31:36) Heb "What is my sin that you have hotly pursued after me." The Hebrew verb translated "pursue hotly" is used elsewhere of soldiers chasing defeated enemies (1 Sam 17:53).
79tn (31:37) Heb "what did you find from all the goods of your house?"
80tn (31:37) Heb "your relatives." The word "relatives" has not been repeated in the translation here for stylistic reasons.
81tn (31:37) Heb "that they may decide between us two."
82tn (31:39) The imperfect verbal form indicates that this was a customary or typical action.
83tn (31:39) Heb "from my hand you exacted it." The imperfect verbal form again indicates that this was a customary or typical action. The words "for every missing animal" are supplied in the translation for clarity; the following clause in Hebrew, "stolen by day or stolen by night," probably means "stolen by wild beasts" and refer to the same animals "torn by wild beasts" in the previous clause, although it may refer to animals stolen by people. The translation used here, "missing," is ambiguous enough to cover either eventuality.
84tn (31:40) Or "by drought."
85tn (31:40) Heb "frost; ice," though when contrasted with the br\j) (j{r#b, "drought; parching heat") of the day, "piercing cold" is more appropriate as a contrast.
86tn (31:40) Heb "and my sleep fled from my eyes."
87tn (31:41) Heb "this to me."
88tn (31:41) Heb "served you," but in this accusatory context the meaning is more "worked like a slave."
89tn (31:42) Heb "the fear of Isaac," that is, the one whom Isaac feared and respected. For further discussion of this title see M. Malul, "More on pahad yishaq (Gen. 31:42,53) and the Oath by the Thigh," VT 35 (1985): 192-200.
90tn (31:42) Heb "My oppression and the work of my hands God saw."
91tn (31:43) Heb "answered and said."
92tn (31:43) Heb "daughters."
93tn (31:43) Heb "children."
94tn (31:43) Heb "but to my daughters what can I do to these today?"
95tn (31:44) Heb "cut a covenant."
96tn (31:44) The verb hy´h* (h*y´h) followed by the preposition l= (l=) means "become."
97tn (31:44) Heb "and it will become a witness between me and you."
98tn (31:46) Heb "Jacob"; the proper name has been replaced by the pronoun ("he") in the translation for stylistic reasons.
99sn (31:46) The Hebrew word for "pile" is G~l (lG~), which sounds like the name "Galeed" (G^lu@d [du@l=G~]). See v. 48.
100sn (31:47) Jegar Sahadutha. Laban the Aramean gave the place an Aramaic name which means "witness pile" or "the pile is a witness."
101sn (31:47) Galeed also means "witness pile" or "the pile is a witness," but this name is Canaanite or Western Semitic and closer to later Hebrew. Jacob, though certainly capable of speaking Aramaic, here prefers to use the western dialect.
102tn (31:48) Heb "a witness between me and you."
103tn (31:49) Heb "and Mizpah."
104sn (31:49) The name Mizpah (m!xP*h [hP*x=m!]), which means "watchpost," sounds like the verb translated "may he watch" (y]x#[ [[x#y]]). Neither Laban nor Jacob felt safe with each other, and so they agreed to go their separate ways, trusting the LORD to keep watch at the border. Jacob did not need this treaty, but Laban, perhaps because he had lost his household gods, felt he did.
105tn (31:49) Heb "between me and you."
106tn (31:49) Heb "for we will be hidden, each man from his neighbor."
107tn (31:50) Heb "see."
108tn (31:50) Heb "between me and you."
109tn (31:51) Heb "and Laban said to Jacob, `Behold this heap and behold the pillar which I have set between men and you.'" The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
110tn (31:52) Heb "This pile is a witness and the pillar is a witness, if I go past this pile to you and if you go past this pile and this pillar to me for harm."
111tn (31:53) The God of Abraham and the god of Nahor. The Hebrew verb translated "judge" is plural, suggesting that Laban has more than one "god" in mind. The Samaritan Pentateuch and the LXX, apparently in an effort to make the statement monotheistic, have a singular verb. In this case one could translate, "May the God of Abraham and the God of Nahor, the God of their father, judge between us." However, Laban had a polytheistic world view, as evidenced by his possession of household idols (cf. 31:19). The translation uses "God" when referring to Abraham's God, for Genesis makes it clear that Abraham worshiped the one true God. It employs "god" when referring to Nahor's god, for in the Hebrew text Laban refers to a different god here, probably one of the local deities.
112tn (31:53) Heb "by the fear of his father Isaac." See the note on the word "fears" in v. 42.
113tn (31:54) The construction is a cognate accusative with the verb, expressing a specific sacrifice.
114tn (31:54) Heb "bread, food." Presumably this was a type of peace offering, where the person bringing the offering ate the animal being sacrificed.
115sn (32:1) Beginning with 31:55, the verse numbers in the English Bible through 32:32 differ by one from the verse numbers in the Hebrew text (BHS), with 31:55 ET = 32:1 HT, 32:1 ET = 32:2 HT, etc., through 32:32 ET = 32:33 HT. From 33:1 the verse numbers in the ET and HT are again the same.
116tn (32:1) Heb "and Laban got up early in the morning and he kissed."
117tn (32:1) Heb "his sons."
118tn (32:1) Heb "to his place."
1sn (32:1) The phrase angels of God occurs only here and in Gen 28:12 in the OT. Jacob saw a vision of angels just before he left the promised land. Now he encounters angels as he prepares to return to it. The text does not give the details of the encounter, but Jacob's response suggests it was amicable. This location was a spot where heaven made contact with earth, and where God made his presence known to the patriarch. See C. Houtman, "Jacob at Mahanaim: Some Remarks on Genesis XXXII 2-3," VT 28 (1978): 37-44.
2tn (32:2) Heb "and Jacob said when he saw them."
3sn (32:2) The name Mahanaim apparently means "two camps." Perhaps the two camps were those of God and of Jacob.
4tn (32:3) Heb "before him."
5tn (32:3) Heb "field."
6sn (32:4) Your servant. The narrative recounts Jacob's groveling in fear before Esau as he calls his brother his "lord," as if to minimize what had been done twenty years ago.
7tn (32:5) Or "I am sending." The form is a preterite with the vav consecutive; it could be rendered as an English present tense--as the Hebrew perfect/preterite allows--much like an epistolary aorist in Greek. The form assumes the temporal perspective of the one who reads the message.
8tn (32:5) The words "this message" are not in the Hebrew text, but have been supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
9tn (32:8) Heb "If Esau comes to one camp and attacks it."
10tn (32:8) Heb "and he said, `If Esau comes to one camp and attacks it." The Hebrew verb rm^a* (a*m^r) here represents Jacob's thought or reasoning, and is therefore translated "he thought." The order of the introductory clause and the direct discourse has been rearranged in the translation for stylistic reasons.
11tn (32:8) Heb "the surviving camp will be for escape." The word "escape" is a feminine noun. The term most often refers to refugees from war.
12tn (32:9) Heb "said."
13tn (32:9) Heb "the one who said."
14tn (32:9) Heb "I will cause good" or "I will treat well [or, "favorably"]." The idea includes more than prosperity, though that is its essential meaning. Here the form is subordinated to the preceding imperative and indicates purpose or result. Jacob is reminding God of his promise in the hope that God will honor his word.
15tn (32:10) Heb "the loving deeds and faithfulness" (see 24:27, 49).
16tn (32:10) Heb "you have done with."
17tn (32:10) Heb "for with my staff." The Hebrew word lq@m^ (m^q@l), traditionally translated "staff," has been rendered as "walking stick" because a "staff" in contemporary English refers typically to the support personnel in an organization.
18tn (32:10) Heb "this Jordan."
19tn (32:11) The imperative has the force of a prayer here, not a command.
20tn (32:11) The "hand" here is a metonymy for "power."
21tn (32:11) Heb "from the hand of my brother, from the hand of Esau."
22tn (32:11) Heb "for I am afraid of him, lest he come."
23sn (32:11) Heb "me, [the] mother upon [the] sons." The first person pronoun "me" probably means here "me and mine," as the following clause suggests.
24tn (32:12) Heb "But you, you said." One of the occurrences of the pronoun "you" has been left untranslated for stylistic reasons.
sn (32:12) Some commentators have thought this final verse of the prayer redundant, but it actually follows the predominant form of a lament in which God is motivated to act. The primary motivation Jacob can offer to God is God's promise, and so he falls back on that at the end of the prayer.
25tn (32:12) Or "will certainly deal well with you." The infinitive absolute appears before the imperfect, underscoring God's promise to bless. The statement is more emphatic than in v. 9.
26tn (32:12) The form is the perfect tense with a vav consecutive, carrying the nuance of the preceding verb forward.
27tn (32:12) Heb "which cannot be counted because of abundance." The imperfect verbal form indicates potential here.
28tn (32:13) Heb "and he"; the referent (Jacob) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
29tn (32:13) Heb "and he took from that which was going into his hand," meaning that he took some of what belonged to him.
30sn (32:13) The Hebrew noun translated gift can in some contexts refer to the tribute paid by a subject to his lord. Such a nuance is possible here, because Jacob refers to Esau as his lord and to himself as Esau's servant (v. 4).
31tn (32:16) Heb "and he put them in the hand of."
32tn (32:16) Heb "a herd, a herd, by itself," or "each herd by itself." The distributive sense is expressed by repetition.
33tn (32:17) Heb "the first"; this has been specified as "the servant leading the first herd" in the translation for clarity.
